class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko" style="font-size: 1rem;"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%8E" title="wikt:李">李</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%99%BD" title="wikt:白">白</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/McCune%E2%80%93Reischauer" title="McCune–Reischauer">McCune–Reischauer</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Korean-language romanization"><span lang="ko-Latn" style="font-style: normal">I P'aek</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">Hiragana</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">りはく</span></span></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><span lang="ja-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Ri Haku</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Li Bai</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">李白</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><i>Lǐ Bái</i></span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">Pe̍h-ōe-jī</a>: <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Lí Pe̍k</span></i>, 701–762), also known as <b>Li Bo</b>, <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a> <b>Taibai</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">太白</span>), <a href="/wiki/Art_name" title="Art name">art name</a> <b>Qinglian Jushi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">青蓮居士</span>), was a Chinese poet, acclaimed from his own time to the present as a brilliant and romantic figure who took traditional poetic forms to new heights. He and his friend <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a> (712–770) were two of the most prominent figures in the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">Chinese poetry</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, which is often called the "<a href="/wiki/Tang_poetry#High_Tang" title="Tang poetry">Golden Age of Chinese Poetry</a>". The expression "Three Wonders" denotes Li Bai's poetry, <a href="/wiki/Pei_Min" title="Pei Min">Pei Min</a>'s swordplay, and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xu" title="Zhang Xu">Zhang Xu</a>'s calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around a thousand poems attributed to him are extant. His poems have been collected into the most important Tang dynasty poetry, <i>Heyaue yingling ji</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> compiled in 753 by Yin Fan. Thirty-four of Li Bai’s poems are included in the anthology <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Hundred_Tang_Poems" title="Three Hundred Tang Poems">Three Hundred Tang Poems</a></i>, which was first published in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Around the same time, translations of his poems began to appear in Europe. The poems were models for celebrating the pleasures of friendship, the depth of nature, solitude, and the joys of drinking wine. Among the most famous are "Waking from Drunkenness on a Spring Day", "The Hard Road to Shu", and "<a href="/wiki/Quiet_Night_Thought" title="Quiet Night Thought">Quiet Night Thought</a>", which are still taught in schoolbooks in China. In the West, multilingual translations of Li's poems continue to be made. His life has even taken on a legendary aspect, including tales of drunkenness, chivalry, and the well-known tale that Li drowned when he reached from his boat to grasp the moon's reflection in the river while drunk.
</p><p>Much of Li's life is reflected in his poetry, the poems are about places he visited, friends whom he saw off on journeys to distant locations perhaps never to meet again, his own dream-like imaginations embroidered with shamanic overtones, current events of which he had news, descriptions taken from nature in a timeless moment of poetry, and so on. However, of particular importance are the changes in the times through which he lived. His early poetry took place in the context of a "golden age" of internal peace and prosperity in the Tang dynasty, under the reign of an emperor who actively promoted and participated in the arts. This ended with the beginning with the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">rebellion</a> of general <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a>. His rebellion lead most of Northern China to be devastated by war and famine. Li's poetry during this period has taken new tones and qualities. Unlike his younger friend Du Fu, Li did not live to see the end of the chaos. However, much of Li's poetry has survived, retaining enduring popularity in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Li Bai is depicted in the <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_Shuang_Pu" title="Wu Shuang Pu">Wu Shuang Pu</a></i> (無雙譜, <i>Table of Peerless Heroes</i>) by Jin Guliang.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table bgcolor="#f7f8ff" cellpadding="3px" cellspacing="0px" style="float:right; border:1px solid; margin:5px">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background:#ccf; border-bottom:1px solid"><a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Names</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td align="right">Chinese:</td>
<td><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hani">李白</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="right"><a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a>:</td>
<td><i>Lǐbaí</i> <i>or</i> Li Bo
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="right"><a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">Zi</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hani">字</span></span>):</td>
<td>Taìbaí (Tai-pai; <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hani">太白</span></span>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="right"><a href="/wiki/Art_name" title="Art name">Hao</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">號</span></span>):</td>
<td>Qinglian <a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">Jushi</a> (Ch'ing-lien <a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">Chu-shih</a>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">青蓮居士</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">青莲居士</span>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td align="right" valign="top">aka:</td>
<td>Shixian (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">詩仙</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">诗仙</span>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Shih-hsien</span></i>)<br/> The Poet Saint <br/> Immortal Poet
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Li Bai's name has been romanized as Li Bai, Li Po, Li Bo (romanizations of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> pronunciations), and Ri Haku (a romanization of the Japanese pronunciation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The varying Chinese romanizations are due to the facts that his given name (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%99%BD" title="wikt:白">白</a>) has two pronunciations in <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_colloquial_readings_of_Chinese_characters" title="Literary and colloquial readings of Chinese characters">literary reading</a> <i>bó</i> (<a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">po<sup>2</sup></span></i>) and the colloquial reading <i>bái</i>; and that earlier authors used <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a> while modern authors prefer <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>. The reconstructed version of how he and others during the Tang dynasty would have pronounced this is <i>Bhæk</i>. His <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a> was Taibai (太白), literally "Great White", as the planet <a href="/wiki/Mount_Taibai#History" title="Mount Taibai">Venus</a> was called at the time. This has been romanized variously as <i>Li Taibo</i>, <i>Li Taibai</i>, <i>Li Tai-po</i>, among others. The Japanese pronunciation of his name and courtesy name may be romanized as "Ri Haku" and "Ri Taihaku" respectively.
</p><p>He is also known by his art name (<i><a href="/wiki/Art_name" title="Art name">hao</a></i>) <i>Qīnglián Jūshì</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">青蓮<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B1%85%E5%A3%AB" title="wikt:居士">居士</a></span></span>), meaning <i><a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">Householder</a> of Azure Lotus</i>, or by the nicknames  "Immortal Poet" (Poet <a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">Transcendent</a>; Wine Immortal (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">酒仙</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Jiuxiān</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Chiu<sup>3</sup>-hsien<sup>1</sup></span></i>), Banished Transcendent (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">謫仙人</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhéxiānrén</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Che<sup>2</sup>-hsien<sup>1</sup>-jen<sup>2</sup></span></i>), Poet-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_knight-errant" title="Chinese knight-errant">Knight-errant</a> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">詩俠</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">诗侠</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Shīxiá</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Shih<sup>1</sup>-hsia<sup>2</sup></span></i>, or "Poet-Hero").
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1964" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg/220px-Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg/330px-Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg/440px-Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nanling_Wushuang_Pu_-_Li_Qinglian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Li Bai, as depicted in the <i>Nanling <a href="/wiki/Wu_Shuang_Pu" title="Wu Shuang Pu">Wushuang Pu</a></i> by Jin Guliang, Ming dynasty</div></div></div>
<p>The two "Books of Tang", <a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang"><i>The Old Book of Tang</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang"><i>The New Book of Tang</i></a>, remain the primary sources of bibliographical material on Li Bai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Other sources include internal evidence from poems by or about Li Bai, and certain other sources, such as the preface to his collected poems by his relative and literary executor, Li Yangbin.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_birth">Background and birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background and birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Bai is generally considered to have been born in 701, in <a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a> (碎葉) of ancient Chinese Central Asia (present-day <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith,_127_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith,_127-6">[6]</a></sup> where his family had prospered in business at the frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_20_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_20-7">[7]</a></sup> Afterwards, the family under the leadership of his father, Li Ke (李客), moved to <a href="/wiki/Jiangyou" title="Jiangyou">Jiangyou</a> (江油), near modern <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, when the youngster was about five years old. There is some mystery or uncertainty about the circumstances of the family's relocations, due to a lack of legal authorization which would have generally been required to move out of the border regions, especially if one's family had been assigned or exiled there.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Two accounts given by contemporaries <a href="/wiki/Li_Yangbing" title="Li Yangbing">Li Yangbing</a> (a family relative) and Fan Chuanzheng state that Li's family was originally from what is now southwestern <a href="/wiki/Jingning_County,_Gansu" title="Jingning County, Gansu">Jingning County, Gansu</a>. Li's ancestry is traditionally traced back to <a href="/wiki/Li_Gao" title="Li Gao">Li Gao</a>, the noble founder of the state of <a href="/wiki/Western_Liang_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Western Liang (Sixteen Kingdoms)">Western Liang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> This provides some support for Li's own claim to be related to the Li dynastic royal family of the Tang dynasty: the Tang emperors also claimed descent from the Li rulers of West Liang. This family was known as the <a href="/wiki/Longxi_County" title="Longxi County">Longxi</a> Li lineage (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%9A%B4%E8%A5%BF%E6%9D%8E%E6%B0%8F" title="zh:隴西李氏">隴西李氏</a>). Evidence suggests that during the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>, Li's own ancestors, at that time for some reason classified socially as commoners, were forced into a form of exile from their original home (in what is now Gansu) to some location or locations further west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> During their exile in the far west, the Li family lived in the ancient <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> city of Suiye (<a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a>, now an archeological site in present-day Kyrgyzstan), and perhaps also in Tiaozhi (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">条枝</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">條枝</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Tiáozhī</span></i>), a state near modern <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazni</a>, Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>  These areas were on the ancient <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, and the Li family were likely merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Their business was quite prosperous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In one <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographic</a> account, while Li Bai's mother was pregnant with him, she had a dream of a great white star falling from heaven. This seems to have contributed to the idea of his being a banished immortal (one of his nicknames).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> That the Great White Star was synonymous with Venus helps to explain his courtesy name: "Tai Bai", or "Venus".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li is known to have married four times. His first marriage, in 727, in <a href="/wiki/Anlu" title="Anlu">Anlu</a>, Hubei, was to the granddaughter of a former government minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_20_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_20-7">[7]</a></sup> His wife was from the well-connected <a href="/wiki/Wu_(surname)" title="Wu (surname)">Wú</a> (吳) family. Li Bai made this his home for about ten years, living in a home owned by his wife's family on Mt. Bishan (碧山).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In 744, he married for the second time in what now is the <a href="/wiki/Liangyuan_District" title="Liangyuan District">Liangyuan District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>. This marriage was to another poet, surnamed <a href="/wiki/Zong_(surname)" title="Zong (surname)">Zong</a> (宗), with whom he both had children<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and exchanges of poems, including many expressions of love for her and their children. His wife, Zong, was a granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Zong_Chuke" title="Zong Chuke">Zong Chuke</a> (宗楚客, died 710), an important government official during the Tang dynasty and the interregnal period of <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Wu Zetian</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 705, when Li Bai was four years old, his father secretly moved his family to <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, where he spent his childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_58_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_58-16">[16]</a></sup> Currently, there is a monument commemorating this in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhongba_Town" title="Zhongba Town">Zhongba Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangyou" title="Jiangyou">Jiangyou</a>, Sichuan province (the area of the modern province then being known as Shu, after a former independent state which had been annexed by the Sui dynasty and later incorporated into the Tang dynasty lands). The young Li spent most of his growing years in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Qinglian,_Jiangyou&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Qinglian, Jiangyou (page does not exist)">Qinglian</a> (青莲; lit. "Blue [also translated as 'green', 'azure', or 'nature-coloured'] Lotus"), a town in Chang-ming County, Sichuan, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_20_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_20-7">[7]</a></sup> This now nominally corresponds with Qinglian Town (青蓮鎮) of <a href="/wiki/Jiangyou" title="Jiangyou">Jiangyou</a> <a href="/wiki/County-level_city" title="County-level city">County-level city</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>.
</p><p>The young Li read extensively, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_classics" title="Confucian classics">Confucian classics</a> such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Songs_(Chinese)" title="Book of Songs (Chinese)">The Classic of Poetry (Shijing)</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_History" title="Book of History">Classic of History (Shujing)</a></i>, as well as various astrological and metaphysical materials which Confucians tended to eschew, though he disdained to take the literacy exam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_58_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_58-16">[16]</a></sup> Reading the "Hundred Authors" was part of the family literary tradition, and he was also able to compose poetry before he was ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_20_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_20-7">[7]</a></sup> The young Li also engaged in other activities, such as taming wild birds and fencing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_58_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_58-16">[16]</a></sup> His other activities included riding, hunting, traveling, and aiding the poor or oppressed by means of both money and arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_20_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_20-7">[7]</a></sup> Eventually, the young Li seems to have become quite skilled in <a href="/wiki/Swordsmanship" title="Swordsmanship">swordsmanship</a>; as this autobiographical quote by Li himself both testifies to and also helps to illustrate the wild life that he led in the Sichuan of his youth:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When I was fifteen, I was fond of sword play, and with that art I challenged quite a few great men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Before he was twenty, Li had fought and killed several men, apparently for reasons of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>, in accordance with the knight-errant tradition (<i><a href="/wiki/Youxia" title="Youxia">youxia</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_58_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_58-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 720, he was interviewed by Governor Su Ting, who considered him a genius. Though he expressed the wish to become an official, he never took the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_civil_service_examination" title="Chinese civil service examination">civil service examination</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="On_the_way_to_Chang.27an"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_way_to_Chang'an">On the way to Chang'an</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: On the way to Chang'an">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dufuschina.jpg"><img alt="Map of eastern interior Chinese cities of Luoyang, Chang'an, Qinzhou, Chengdu, Kuizhou, and Tanzhou" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="471" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Dufuschina.jpg/300px-Dufuschina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Dufuschina.jpg/450px-Dufuschina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Dufuschina.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dufuschina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The China of Li Bai and Du Fu</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaving_Sichuan">Leaving Sichuan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Leaving Sichuan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In his mid-twenties, about 725, Li Bai left Sichuan, sailing down the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangzi_River" title="Yangzi River">Yangzi River</a> through <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>, beginning his days of wandering. He then went back up-river, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yunmeng" title="Yunmeng">Yunmeng</a>, in what is now <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>, where his marriage to the granddaughter of a retired prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Xu_Yushi" title="Xu Yushi">Xu Yushi</a>, seems to have formed but a brief interlude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> During the first year of his trip, he met celebrities and gave away much of his wealth to needy friends.
</p><p>In 730, Li Bai stayed at Zhongnan Mountain near the capital Chang'an (Xi'an), and tried but failed to secure a position. He sailed down the Yellow River, stopped by Luoyang, and visited Taiyuan before going home. In 735, Li Bai was in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, where he intervened in a court martial against <a href="/wiki/Guo_Ziyi" title="Guo Ziyi">Guo Ziyi</a>, who was later, after becoming one of the top Tang generals, to repay the favour during the An Shi disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> By perhaps 740, he had moved to <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>. It was in Shandong at this time that he became one of the group known as the "Six Idlers of the Bamboo Brook", an informal group dedicated to literature and wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> He wandered about the area of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, eventually making friends with a famous Daoist priest, Wu Yun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> In 742, Wu Yun was summoned by the Emperor to attend the imperial court, where his praise of Li Bai was great.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="At_Chang.27an"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="At_Chang'an">At Chang'an</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: At Chang'an">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wu Yun's praise of Li Bai led <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a> (born Li Longji and also known as Emperor Minghuang) to summon Li to the court in <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>. Li's personality fascinated the aristocrats and common people alike, including another Taoist (and poet), <a href="/wiki/He_Zhizhang" title="He Zhizhang">He Zhizhang</a>, who bestowed upon him the nickname the "Immortal Exiled from Heaven".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> Indeed, after an initial audience, where Li Bai was questioned about his political views, the Emperor was so impressed that he held a big banquet in his honor. At this banquet, the Emperor was said to show his favor, even to the extent of personally seasoning his soup for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emperor Xuanzong employed him as a translator, as Li Bai knew at least one non-Chinese language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_59_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_59-13">[13]</a></sup> Ming Huang eventually gave him a post at the <a href="/wiki/Hanlin_Academy" title="Hanlin Academy">Hanlin Academy</a>, which served to provide scholarly expertise and poetry for the Emperor.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg/250px-L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg/375px-L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg/500px-L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27empereur_Minghuang_regardant_Li_Bai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor Minghuang, seated on a terrace, observes Li Bai write poetry while having his boots taken off (Qing dynasty illustration).</div></div></div><p>When the emperor ordered Li Bai to the palace, he was often drunk, but quite capable of performing on the spot.
</p><p>Li Bai wrote several poems about the Emperor's beautiful and beloved <a href="/wiki/Yang_Guifei" title="Yang Guifei">Yang Guifei</a>, the favorite royal consort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_60_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_60-20">[20]</a></sup> A story, probably apocryphal, circulates about Li Bai during this period. Once, while drunk, Li Bai had gotten his boots muddy, and <a href="/wiki/Gao_Lishi" title="Gao Lishi">Gao Lishi</a>, the most politically powerful <a href="/wiki/Eunuch#China" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a> in the palace, was asked to assist in the removal of these, in front of the Emperor. Gao took offense at being asked to perform this menial service, and later managed to persuade Yang Guifei to take offense at Li's poems concerning her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_60_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_60-20">[20]</a></sup> At the persuasion of Yang Guifei and Gao Lishi, Xuanzong reluctantly, but politely, and with large gifts of gold and silver, sent Li Bai away from the royal court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> After leaving the court, Li Bai formally became a Taoist, making a home in <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>, but wandering far and wide for the next ten some years, writing poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> Li Bai lived and wrote poems at Bishan (or Bi Mountain (碧山), today Baizhao Mountain (白兆山)) in <a href="/wiki/Yandian,_Hubei" title="Yandian, Hubei">Yandian, Hubei</a>.  Bi Mountain (碧山) in the poem <i>Question and Answer Amongst the Mountains</i> (山中问答 Shanzhong Wenda) refers to this mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-govyd_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-govyd-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meeting_Du_Fu">Meeting Du Fu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Meeting Du Fu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a></div>
<p>He met <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a> in the autumn of 744, when they shared a single room and various activities together, such as traveling, hunting, wine, and poetry, thus established a close and lasting friendship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_24_and_25_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_24_and_25-23">[23]</a></sup> They met again the following year. These were the only occasions on which they met, in person, although they continued to maintain a relationship through poetry. This is reflected in the dozen or so poems by Du Fu to or about Li Bai which survive, and the one from Li Bai directed toward Du Fu which remains.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_and_exile">War and exile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: War and exile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Riders_on_Horseback,_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1273" data-file-width="1353" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Riders_on_Horseback%2C_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Riders_on_Horseback,_Northern_Qi_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Riders on Horseback, Northern Qi Dynasty, the general area of the rebel heartland, although of an earlier date.</div></div></div>
<p>At the end of 755, the disorders instigated by the rebel general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_Shi_Rebellion" title="An Shi Rebellion">An Lushan</a> burst across the land. The Emperor eventually fled to Sichuan and abdicated. During the confusion, the Crown Prince opportunely declared himself Emperor and head of the government. The An Shi disturbances continued (as they were later called, since they lasted beyond the death of their instigator, carried on by <a href="/wiki/Shi_Siming" title="Shi Siming">Shi Siming</a> and others). Li Bai became a staff adviser to Prince Yong, one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Huang" title="Ming Huang">Ming Huang</a>'s (Emperor Xuanzong's) sons, who was far from the top of the primogeniture list, yet named to share the imperial power as a general after Xuanzong had abdicated, in 756.
</p><p>However, even before the empire's external enemies were defeated, the two brothers fell to fighting each other with their armies. Upon the defeat of the Prince's forces by his brother the new emperor in 757, Li Bai escaped, but was later captured, imprisoned in <a href="/wiki/Jiujiang" title="Jiujiang">Jiujiang</a>, and sentenced to death. The famous and powerful army general <a href="/wiki/Guo_Ziyi" title="Guo Ziyi">Guo Ziyi</a> and others intervened; Guo Ziyi was the very person whom Li Bai had saved from court martial a couple of decades before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> His wife, the lady Zong, and others (such as Song Ruosi) wrote petitions for clemency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_26_and_27_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_26_and_27-24">[24]</a></sup> Upon General Guo Ziyi's offering to exchange his official rank for Li Bai's life, Li Bai's death sentence was commuted to exile: he was consigned to <a href="/wiki/Yelang" title="Yelang">Yelang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> Yelang (in what is now <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>) was in the remote extreme southwestern part of the empire, and was considered to be outside the main sphere of Chinese civilization and culture. Li Bai headed toward Yelang with little sign of hurry, stopping for prolonged social visits (sometimes for months), and writing poetry along the way, leaving detailed descriptions of his journey for posterity. Notice of an imperial pardon recalling Li Bai reached him before he even got near Yelang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> He had only gotten as far as <a href="/wiki/Wu_Gorge" title="Wu Gorge">Wushan</a>, when news of his pardon caught up with him in 759.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_26_and_27_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_26_and_27-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_and_other_travels">Return and other travels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Return and other travels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When Li received the news of his imperial reprieve, he returned down the river to <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, passing on the way through <a href="/wiki/Baidicheng" title="Baidicheng">Baidicheng</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Kuizhou" title="Kuizhou">Kuizhou</a> Prefecture, still engaging in the pleasures of food, wine, good company, and writing poetry; his poem "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Translation:Departing_from_Baidi_in_the_Morning" title="s:Translation:Departing from Baidi in the Morning">Departing from Baidi in the Morning</a>" records this stage of his travels, as well as poetically mocking his enemies and detractors, implied in his inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Simians_(Chinese_poetry)" title="Simians (Chinese poetry)">imagery of monkeys</a>. Although Li did not cease his wandering lifestyle, he then generally confined his travels to <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and the two <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Xuancheng" title="Xuancheng">Xuancheng</a> and Li Yang (in modern <a href="/wiki/Zhao_County" title="Zhao County">Zhao County</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> His poems of this time include nature poems and poems of socio-political protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun,_24_and_25_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sun,_24_and_25-23">[23]</a></sup> Eventually, in 762, Li's relative <a href="/wiki/Li_Yangbing" title="Li Yangbing">Li Yangbing</a> became magistrate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangtu" title="Dangtu">Dangtu</a>, and Li Bai went to stay with him there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> In the meantime, Suzong and Xuanzong both died within a short period of time, and China had a new emperor. Also, China was involved in renewed efforts to suppress further military disorders stemming from the Anshi rebellions, and Li volunteered to serve on the general staff of the Chinese commander <a href="/wiki/Li_Guangbi" title="Li Guangbi">Li Guangbi</a>. However, at age 61, Li became critically ill, and his health would not allow him to fulfill this plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg/220px-LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg/330px-LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg/440px-LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LiBaimemorialhall000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Li Bai Memorial Hall in <a href="/wiki/Jiangyou" title="Jiangyou">Jiangyou</a>, Sichuan</div></div></div>
<p>The new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Daizong" title="Emperor Daizong">Emperor Daizong</a> named Li Bai the Registrar of the Left Commandant's office in 762. However, by the time that the imperial edict arrived in <a href="/wiki/Dangtu_County" title="Dangtu County">Dangtu, Anhui</a>, Li Bai was already dead.
</p><p>There is a long and sometimes fanciful tradition regarding his death, from uncertain Chinese sources, that Li Bai drowned after falling from his boat one day he had gotten very drunk as he tried to embrace the reflection of the moon in the Yangtze River, something later believed by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Giles" title="Herbert Giles">Herbert Giles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu,_61_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-Wu,_61-21">[21]</a></sup> However, the actual cause appears to have been natural enough, although perhaps related to his hard-living lifestyle. Nevertheless, the legend has a place in Chinese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>A memorial of Li Bai lies just west of <a href="/wiki/Ma%27anshan" title="Ma'anshan">Ma'anshan</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calligraphy">Calligraphy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Calligraphy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libai_shangyangtai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Libai_shangyangtai.jpg/400px-Libai_shangyangtai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Libai_shangyangtai.jpg/600px-Libai_shangyangtai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Libai_shangyangtai.jpg/800px-Libai_shangyangtai.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libai_shangyangtai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The only surviving calligraphy in Li Bai's own handwriting, titled <i>Shangyangtai</i> (To Yangtai Temple), located at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_Museum" title="Palace Museum">Palace Museum</a> in Beijing, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-mag-27">[27]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Li Bai was also a skilled <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligrapher</a>, though there is only one surviving piece of his calligraphy work in his own handwriting that exists today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag-27">[27]</a></sup> The piece is titled <i>Shàng yáng tái</i> (<i>Going Up To Sun Terrace</i>), a 38.1 by 28.5 centimetres (15.0 in × 11.2 in) long scroll (with later addition of a title written by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Huizong of Song">Emperor Huizong of Song</a> and a postscript added by <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> himself); the calligraphy is housed in the <a href="/wiki/Palace_Museum" title="Palace Museum">Palace Museum</a> in Beijing, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arts_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-arts-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_texts_and_editing">Surviving texts and editing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Surviving texts and editing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even Li Bai and Du Fu, the two most famous and most comprehensively edited Tang poets, were affected by the destruction of the imperial Tang libraries and the loss of many private collections in the periods of turmoil (<a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huang_Chao" title="Huang Chao">Huang Chao</a> Rebellion). Although many of Li Bai's poems have survived, even more were lost and there is difficulty regarding variant texts. One of the earliest endeavors at editing Li Bai's work was by his relative <a href="/wiki/Li_Yangbing" title="Li Yangbing">Li Yangbing</a>, the magistrate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangtu" title="Dangtu">Dangtu</a>, with whom he stayed in his final years and to whom he entrusted his manuscripts. However, the most reliable texts are not necessarily in the earliest editions. Song dynasty scholars produced various editions of his poetry, but it was not until the Qing dynasty that such collections as the <i><a href="/wiki/Quan_Tangshi" title="Quan Tangshi">Quan Tangshi</a></i> (<i>Complete Tang Poems</i>) made the most comprehensive studies of the then surviving texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">Themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics have focused on Li Bai's strong sense of the continuity of poetic tradition, his glorification of alcoholic beverages (and, indeed, frank celebration of drunkenness), his use of persona, the fantastic extremes of some of his imagery, his mastery of formal poetic rules—and his ability to combine all of these with a seemingly effortless virtuosity to produce inimitable poetry. Other themes in Li's poetry, noted especially in the 20th century, are sympathy for the common folk and antipathy towards needless wars (even when conducted by the emperor himself).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetic_tradition">Poetic tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Poetic tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Bai had a strong sense of himself as being part of a poetic tradition. The "genius" of Li Bai, says one recent account, "lies at once in his total command of the literary tradition before him and his ingenuity in bending (without breaking) it to discover a uniquely personal idiom..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burton_Watson" title="Burton Watson">Burton Watson</a>, comparing him to Du Fu, says Li's poetry, "is essentially backward-looking, that it represents more a revival and fulfillment of past promises and glory than a foray into the future."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_141_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_141-32">[32]</a></sup> Watson adds, as evidence, that of all the poems attributed to Li Bai, about one sixth are in the form of <i><a href="/wiki/Yuefu" title="Yuefu">yuefu</a></i>, or, in other words, reworked lyrics from traditional folk ballads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> As further evidence, Watson cites the existence of a fifty-nine poem collection by Li Bai entitled <i>Gu Feng</i>, or <i>In the Old Manner</i>, which is, in part, tribute to the poetry of the Han and Wei dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_142_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_142-34">[34]</a></sup> His admiration for certain particular poets is also shown through specific allusions, for example to <a href="/wiki/Qu_Yuan" title="Qu Yuan">Qu Yuan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tao_Yuanming" title="Tao Yuanming">Tao Yuanming</a>, and occasionally by name, for example Du Fu.
</p><p>A more general appreciation for history is shown on the part of Li Bai in his poems of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huaigu_(poetry)" title="Huaigu (poetry)">huaigu</a></i> genre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> or meditations on the past, wherein following "one of the perennial themes of Chinese poetry", "the poet contemplates the ruins of past glory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rapt_with_wine_and_moon">Rapt with wine and moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rapt with wine and moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinesericewine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chinesericewine.jpg/220px-Chinesericewine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chinesericewine.jpg/330px-Chinesericewine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chinesericewine.jpg/440px-Chinesericewine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinesericewine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese rice wine</div></div></div>
<p>John C. H. Wu observed that "while some may have drunk more wine than Li [Bai], no-one has written more poems about wine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Classical Chinese poets were often associated with drinking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_alcoholic_beverages" title="Chinese alcoholic beverages">wine</a>, and Li Bai was part of the group of Chinese scholars in Chang'an his fellow poet <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a> called the "<a href="/wiki/Eight_Immortals_of_the_Wine_Cup" title="Eight Immortals of the Wine Cup">Eight Immortals of the Wine Cup</a>." The Chinese generally did not find the moderate use of alcohol to be immoral or unhealthy. James J. Y Liu comments that  <i>zui</i>  in poetry "does not mean quite the same thing as 'drunk', 'intoxicated', or 'inebriated', but rather means being mentally carried away from one's normal preoccupations ..." Liu translates <i>zui</i> as "rapt with wine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The "Eight Immortals", however, drank to an unusual degree, though they still were viewed as pleasant eccentrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Burton Watson concluded that "[n]early all Chinese poets celebrate the joys of wine, but none so tirelessly and with such a note of genuine conviction as Li [Bai]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_143_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_143-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Li Bai's most famous poems is "Waking From Drunkenness on a Spring Day" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">春日醉起言志</span></span>). <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waley" title="Arthur Waley">Arthur Waley</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> translated it as follows:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><i><b>Waking From Drunkenness on a Spring Day</b></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">春日醉起言志</span></span>)<br/>
<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">處世若大夢,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Life in the world is but a big dream;<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">胡爲勞其生.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>I will not spoil it by any labour or care.<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">所以終日醉,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>So saying, I was drunk all the day,<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">頹然臥前楹.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Lying helpless at the porch in front of my door.<br/>
<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">覺來盼庭前,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>When I awoke, I blinked at the garden-lawn;<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">一鳥花間鳴.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>A lonely bird was singing amid the flowers.<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">借問此何時,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>I asked myself, had the day been wet or fine?<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">春風語流鶯.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>The Spring wind was telling the mango-bird.<br/>
<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">感之欲嘆息,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Moved by its song I soon began to sigh,<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">對酒還自傾.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>And, as wine was there, I filled my own cup.<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">浩歌待明月,</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Wildly singing I waited for the moon to rise;<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">曲盡已忘情.</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>When my song was over, all my senses had gone.
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fantastic_imagery">Fantastic imagery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fantastic imagery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An important characteristic of Li Bai's poetry "is the fantasy and note of childlike wonder and playfulness that pervade so much of it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_142_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_142-34">[34]</a></sup> Burton Watson attributes this to a fascination with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_priest" title="Taoist priest">Taoist priest</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a> recluses who practiced alchemy and austerities in the mountains, in the aim of becoming <a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a>, or immortal beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_142_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_142-34">[34]</a></sup> There is a strong element of Taoism in his works, both in the sentiments they express and in their spontaneous tone, and "many of his poems deal with mountains, often descriptions of ascents that midway modulate into journeys of the imagination, passing from actual mountain scenery to visions of nature deities, immortals, and 'jade maidens' of Taoist lore".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_142_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_142-34">[34]</a></sup> Watson sees this as another affirmation of Li Bai's affinity with the past, and a continuity with the traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chuci" title="Chuci">Chuci</a> and the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu_(literature)" title="Fu (literature)">fu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_143_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_143-40">[40]</a></sup> Watson finds this "element of fantasy" to be behind Li Bai's use of <a href="/wiki/Hyperbole" title="Hyperbole">hyperbole</a> and the "playful personifications" of mountains and celestial objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_143_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_143-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nostalgia">Nostalgia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nostalgia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The critic James J.Y. Liu notes "Chinese poets seem to be perpetually bewailing their exile and longing to return home. This may seem sentimental to Western readers, but one should remember the vastness of China, the difficulties of communication... the sharp contrast between the highly cultured life in the main cities and the harsh conditions in the remoter regions of the country, and the importance of family..." It is hardly surprising, he concludes, that nostalgia should have become a "constant, and hence conventional, theme in Chinese poetry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Liu gives as a prime example Li's poem "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Quiet_Night_Thought" title="A Quiet Night Thought">A Quiet Night Thought</a>" (also translated as "Contemplating Moonlight"), which is often learned by schoolchildren in China.  In a mere 20 words, the poem uses the vivid moonlight and frost imagery to convey the feeling of <a href="/wiki/Homesickness" title="Homesickness">homesickness</a>.  This translation is by <a href="/wiki/Yang_Xianyi" title="Yang Xianyi">Yang Xianyi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dai_Naidie" title="Dai Naidie">Dai Naidie</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><b>Thoughts in the Silent Night</b> (<i>Jìngyè Sī</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">静夜思</span></span>)<br/>
<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">床前明月光，</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Beside my bed a pool of light—<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">疑是地上霜，</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>Is it hoarfrost on the ground?<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">舉頭望明月，</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>I lift my eyes and see the moon,<br/>
<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">低頭思故鄉。</span></span><span class="nowrap">   </span>I lower my face and think of home.
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_persona">Use of persona</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Use of persona">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Bai also wrote a number of poems from various viewpoints, including the <a href="/wiki/Persona" title="Persona">personae</a> of women. For example, he wrote several poems in the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Songs_poetry" title="Midnight Songs poetry"><i>Zi Ye</i>, or "Lady Midnight" style</a>, as well as Han folk-ballad style poems.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_virtuosity">Technical virtuosity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Technical virtuosity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Bai is well known for the technical virtuosity of his poetry and the mastery of his verses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_141_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_141-32">[32]</a></sup> In terms of poetic form, "critics generally agree that Li [Bai] produced no significant innovations ... In theme and content also, his poetry is notable less for the new elements it introduces than for the skill with which he brightens the old ones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_141_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_141-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Burton Watson comments on Li Bai's famous poem, which he translates "Bring the Wine": "like so much of Li [Bai]'s work, it has a grace and effortless dignity that somehow make it more compelling than earlier treatment of the same."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Bai's <a href="/wiki/Yuefu" title="Yuefu">yuefu</a> poems have been called the greatest of all time by <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming-dynasty</a> scholar and writer <a href="/wiki/Hu_Yinglin" title="Hu Yinglin">Hu Yinglin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Bai especially excelled in the <a href="/wiki/Gushi_(poetry)" title="Gushi (poetry)">Gushi</a> form, or "old style" poems, a type of poetry allowing a great deal of freedom in terms of the form and content of the work. An example is his poem "蜀道難", translated by Witter Bynner as "Hard Roads in Shu". Shu is a poetic term for Sichuan, the destination of refuge that Emperor Xuanzong considered fleeing to escape the approaching forces of the rebel General <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a>. Watson comments that, this poem, "employs lines that range in length from four to eleven characters, the form of the lines suggesting by their irregularity the jagged peaks and bumpy mountain roads of Sichuan depicted in the poem."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson,_141_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Watson,_141-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Bai was also noted as a master of the <a href="/wiki/Jueju" title="Jueju">jueju</a>, or cut-verse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Ming-dynasty poet Li Pan Long thought Li Bai was the greatest jueju master of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Bai was noted for his mastery of the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCshi_(poetry)" title="Lüshi (poetry)">lüshi</a>, or "regulated verse", the formally most demanding verse form of the times. Watson notes, however, that his poem "Seeing a Friend Off" was "unusual in that it violates the rule that the two middle couplets ... must observe verbal parallelism", adding that Chinese critics excused this kind of violation in the case of a genius like Li.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4394" data-file-width="2228" decoding="async" height="434" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg/220px-Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg/330px-Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg/440px-Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leng_Mei_-_Figures_-_Spring_Evening_Banquet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Spring Evening Banquet at the Peach and Pear Blossom Garden</i> with quoted text by Li Bai, painted by <a href="/wiki/Leng_Mei" title="Leng Mei">Leng Mei</a>, late 17th or early 18th century, <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_Museum" title="National Palace Museum">National Palace Museum</a>, Taipei</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_East">In the East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: In the East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Bai's poetry was immensely influential in his own time, as well as for subsequent generations in China. From early on, he was paired with Du Fu. The recent scholar Paula Varsano observes that "in the literary imagination they were, and remain, the two greatest poets of the Tang—or even of China". Yet she notes the persistence of "what we can rightly call the 'Li-Du debate', the terms of which became so deeply ingrained in the critical discourse surrounding these two poets that almost any characterization of the one implicitly critiqued the other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVarsano2014_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVarsano2014-49">[49]</a></sup> Li's influence has also been demonstrated in the immediate geographical area of Chinese cultural influence, being known as Ri Haku in Japan. This influence continues even today. Examples range from poetry to painting and to literature.
</p><p>In his own lifetime, during his many wanderings and while he was attending court in Chang'an, he met and parted from various contemporary poets. These meetings and separations were typical occasions for versification in the tradition of the literate Chinese of the time, a prime example being his relationship with Du Fu.
</p><p>After his lifetime, his influence continued to grow. Some four centuries later, during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>, for example, just in the case of his poem that is sometimes translated "Drinking Alone Beneath the Moon", the poet <a href="/wiki/Yang_Wanli" title="Yang Wanli">Yang Wanli</a> wrote a whole poem alluding to it (and to two other Li Bai poems), in the same <i>gushi</i>, or old-style poetry form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, Li Bai even influenced the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>.
</p><p>In China, his poem "<a href="/wiki/Quiet_Night_Thought" title="Quiet Night Thought">Quiet Night Thoughts</a>", reflecting a nostalgia of a traveller away from home,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> has been widely  "memorized by school children and quoted by adults".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>He is sometimes worshipped as an immortal in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a> and is also considered a divinity in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> <a href="/wiki/Caodaism" title="Caodaism">Cao Dai religion</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_West">In the West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In the West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Swiss composer <a href="/wiki/Volkmar_Andreae" title="Volkmar Andreae">Volkmar Andreae</a> set eight poems as <i>Li-Tai-Pe: Eight Chinese songs for tenor and orchestra, op. 37</i>. American composer <a href="/wiki/Harry_Partch" title="Harry Partch">Harry Partch</a>, based his <i>Seventeen Lyrics by Li Po</i> for intoning voice and Adapted Viola (an instrument of Partch's own invention) on texts in <i>The Works of Li Po, the Chinese Poet</i> translated by Shigeyoshi Obata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In Brazil, the songwriter Beto Furquim included a musical setting of the poem "Jing Ye Si" in his album "Muito Prazer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ezra_Pound">Ezra Pound</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ezra Pound">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Li Bai is influential in the West partly due to <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>'s versions of some of his poems in the collection <i><a href="/wiki/Cathay_(poetry_collection)" title="Cathay (poetry collection)"><i>Cathay</i></a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pound_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pound-55">[55]</a></sup> (Pound transliterating his name according to the Japanese manner as "Rihaku").  Li Bai's interactions with nature, friendship, his love of wine and his acute observations of life inform his more popular poems. Some, like <i>Changgan xing</i> (translated by <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a> as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_River_Merchant%27s_Wife:_A_Letter" title="s:The River Merchant's Wife: A Letter">"The River Merchant's Wife: A Letter"</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pound_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pound-55">[55]</a></sup> record the hardships or emotions of common people. An example of the liberal, but poetically influential, translations, or adaptations, of Japanese versions of his poems made, largely based on the work of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Fenollosa" title="Ernest Fenollosa">Ernest Fenollosa</a> and professors Mori and Ariga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pound_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pound-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gustav_Mahler">Gustav Mahler</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Gustav Mahler">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Gustav Mahler</a> integrated four of Li Bai's works into his symphonic song cycle <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Lied_von_der_Erde" title="Das Lied von der Erde">Das Lied von der Erde</a></i>. These were derived from free German translations by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethge_(poet)" title="Hans Bethge (poet)">Hans Bethge</a>, published in an anthology called <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Die chinesische Flöte</i></span> (<i>The Chinese Flute</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Bethge based his versions on the collection <i>Chinesische Lyrik</i> by Hans Heilmann (1905). Heilmann worked from pioneering 19th-century translations into French: three by the <a href="/wiki/Marie-Jean-L%C3%A9on,_Marquis_d%27Hervey_de_Saint_Denys" title="Marie-Jean-Léon, Marquis d'Hervey de Saint Denys">Marquis d'Hervey-Saint-Denys</a> and one (only distantly related to the Chinese) by <a href="/wiki/Judith_Gautier" title="Judith Gautier">Judith Gautier</a>. Mahler freely changed Bethge's text.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reference_in_Beat_Generation">Reference in Beat Generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Reference in Beat Generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Li Bai's poetry can be seen as being an influence to <a href="/wiki/Beat_Generation" title="Beat Generation">Beat Generation</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Gary_Snyder" title="Gary Snyder">Gary Snyder</a> during Snyder's years of studying Asian Culture and Zen. Li Bai's style of descriptive writing assisted in the diversity within the Beat writing style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (May 2019)">circular reference</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translation">Translation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Bai&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Translation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Li Bai's poetry was introduced to Europe by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Joseph_Marie_Amiot" title="Jean Joseph Marie Amiot">Jean Joseph Marie Amiot</a>, a Jesuit missionary in Beijing, in his <i>Portraits des Célèbres Chinois</i>, published in the series <i>Mémoires concernant l'histoire, les sciences, les arts, les mœurs, les usages, &amp;c. des Chinois, par les missionnaires de Pekin</i>. (1776–1797).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obata,_v_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Obata,_v-59">[59]</a></sup> Further translations into French were published by <a href="/wiki/Marie-Jean-L%C3%A9on,_Marquis_d%27Hervey_de_Saint_Denys" title="Marie-Jean-Léon, Marquis d'Hervey de Saint Denys">Marquis d'Hervey de Saint-Denys</a> in his 1862 <i>Poésies de l'Époque des Thang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Denys_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Denys-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Edkins" title="Joseph Edkins">Joseph Edkins</a> read a paper, "On Li Tai-po", to the Peking Oriental Society in 1888, which was subsequently published in that society's journal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The early sinologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbert_Allen_Giles" title="Herbert Allen Giles">Herbert Allen Giles</a> included translations of Li Bai in his 1898 publication <i>Chinese Poetry in English Verse</i>, and again in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Chinese_Literature" title="History of Chinese Literature">History of Chinese Literature</a></i> (1901).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The third early translator into English was L. Cranmer-Byng (1872–1945). His <i>Lute of Jade: Being Selections from the Classical Poets of China</i> (1909) and <i>A Feast of Lanterns</i> (1916) both featured Li's poetry.
</p><p>Renditions of Li Bai's poetry into <a href="/wiki/Modernist_poetry_in_English#Imagism" title="Modernist poetry in English">modernist English poetry</a> were influential through <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound#Ripostes,_translation_work" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a> in <i>Cathay</i> (1915) and <a href="/wiki/Amy_Lowell" title="Amy Lowell">Amy Lowell</a> in <i>Fir-Flower Tablets</i> (1921). Neither worked directly from the Chinese: Pound relied on more or less literal, word for word, though not terribly accurate, translations of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Fenollosa" title="Ernest Fenollosa">Ernest Fenollosa</a> and what Pound called the "decipherings" of professors Mori and Ariga; Lowell on those of <a href="/wiki/Florence_Ayscough" title="Florence Ayscough">Florence Ayscough</a>. <a href="/wiki/Witter_Bynner" title="Witter Bynner">Witter Bynner</a> with the help of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiang_Kang-hu" title="Kiang Kang-hu">Kiang Kang-hu</a> included several of Li's poems in <i>The Jade Mountain</i> (1939). Although Li was not his preferred poet, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waley" title="Arthur Waley">Arthur Waley</a> translated a few of his poems into English for the <i>Asiatic Review</i>, and included them in his <i>More Translations from the Chinese</i>. Shigeyoshi Obata, in his 1922 <i>The Works of Li Po</i>, claimed he had made "the first attempt ever made to deal with any single Chinese poet exclusively in one book for the purpose of introducing him to the English-speaking world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Obata,_v_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Obata,_v-59">[59]</a></sup> A translation of Li Bai's poem <i>Green Moss</i> by poet William Carlos Williams was sent as a letter to Chinese American poet David Rafael Wang where Williams was seen as having a similar tone as Pound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Bai became a favorite among translators for his straightforward and seemingly simple style. Later translations are too numerous to discuss here, but an extensive selection of Li's poems, translated by various translators, is included in John Minford and Joseph S. M. Lau, <i>Classical Chinese Literature</i> (2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
